Title: Diffusion Model for Manifold Data: Score Decomposition, Curvature, and Statistical Complexity

URL Source: https://arxiv.org/pdf/2603.20645v1

Published Time: Tue, 24 Mar 2026 00:31:20 GMT

Number of Pages: 86

Markdown Content:
## Diffusion Model for Manifold Data: Score Decomposition, Curvature, and Statistical Complexity 

Zixuan Zhang 1 Kaixuan Huang 2 Tuo Zhao 1 Mengdi Wang 2 Minshuo Chen 31Georgia Tech 2Princeton University 3Northwestern University March 24, 2026 

Abstract 

Diffusion models have become a leading framework in generative modeling, yet their the-oretical understanding—especially for high-dimensional data concentrated on low-dimensional structures—remains incomplete. This paper investigates how diffusion models learn such struc-tured data, focusing on two key aspects: statistical complexity and influence of data geometric properties. By modeling data as samples from a smooth Riemannian manifold, our analysis re-veals crucial decompositions of score functions in diffusion models under different levels of injected noise. We also highlight the interplay of manifold curvature with the structures in the score func-tion. These analyses enable an efficient neural network approximation to the score function, built upon which we further provide statistical rates for score estimation and distribution learning. Re-markably, the obtained statistical rates are governed by the intrinsic dimension of data and the manifold curvature. These results advance the statistical foundations of diffusion models, bridging theory and practice for generative modeling on manifolds. 

1 Introduction 

Diffusion models have recently emerged as a powerful framework in generative modeling, surpassing the performance of traditional methods like Generative Adversarial Networks (GANs, Goodfellow et al. [2014]) and Variational AutoEncoders (VAEs, Kingma and Welling [2013]) in many applica-tions [Ho et al., 2020, Song and Ermon, 2019, Song et al., 2021, Rombach et al., 2022]. Diffusion models have found success across a variety of domains, including image synthesis [Dhariwal and Nichol, 2021, Huang et al., 2025, Song and Ermon, 2019], audio generation [Kong et al., 2020, Yang et al., 2023a], and natural language processing [Austin et al., 2021, Lou et al., 2023, Nie et al., 2025], and have even extended their influence into areas like computational biology [Watson et al., 2023, Guo et al., 2024], reinforcement learning and control [Yang et al., 2023b, Croitoru et al., 2023, Chen et al., 2024]. Despite their empirical success, a comprehensive theoretical understanding of diffusion models—especially in high-dimensional settings—remains limited, and further exploration is essential to advance both their theoretical and practical potential. At their core, diffusion models learn complex data distributions through a pair of processes: a forward process, where Gaussian noise is incrementally added to data until the data distribution converges to white noise, and a backward process, where a denoising neural network learns to reverse this noise corruption. The denoising neural network estimates the so-called score function, which is the gradient of the log-probability density of the (corrupted) data distribution [Ho et al., 2020, Song and Ermon, 2019, Song et al., 2020]. The score function is central to understanding the learned distribution and to the generation of new data samples. 1

> arXiv:2603.20645v1 [cs.LG] 21 Mar 2026

While diffusion models have demonstrated their ability to generate high-quality samples in high-dimensional settings, this success challenges the common expectation that learning in such environ-ments should be hampered by the curse of dimensionality [Tsybakov, 2008, Wasserman, 2006]. A plausible explanation for this success lies in the observation that many real-world datasets exhibit an underlying low-dimensional structure. Although the data may reside in high-dimensional ambi-ent spaces, the intrinsic dimensionality of the data is often much lower, and these low-dimensional structures can often be understood as manifolds [Tenenbaum et al., 2000, Roweis and Saul, 2000]. In many domains, data display regularities and symmetries that suggest they can be modeled on low-dimensional manifolds. For instance, the set of all images of an object taken under different lighting conditions or orientations tends to lie on a low-dimensional manifold within the high-dimensional pixel space. By exploiting such low-dimensional structures, a line of work has shown that neural networks can efficiently approximate and estimate functions supported on these data domains, with performance dependent primarily on the intrinsic dimensionality of the data rather than its ambient dimensionality [Schmidt-Hieber, 2020, Suzuki, 2019, Chen et al., 2022a, Suh et al., 2023, Wang et al., 2023]. While the low-dimensional manifold hypothesis offers an intuitive explanation for the empirical success of diffusion models, fundamental gaps persist in understanding their behavior on manifold data. Rigorous analyses and precise characterizations of statistical complexity—beyond idealized settings such as access to accurate score estimators [De Bortoli, 2022, Huang et al., 2024]—remain underdeveloped. Notable sample complexity bounds are only established in a few very recent works [Tang and Yang, 2024, Azangulov et al., 2024, Yakovlev and Puchkin, 2025, ?]. Yet, in these works, the interplay between the manifold geometry (e.g., curvature and boundary effects) and the performance of diffusion models is largely uncharacterized; see a detailed discussion in Section 1.2. Despite diffusion models excelling at generating globally coherent structures, they often struggle with local fine-scale details. For instance, diffusion models frequently produce globally coherent images with local geometric inconsistencies—such as distorted fingers in synthesized human hands or hallucinated text in generated documents [Lu et al., 2025, 2024, Aithal et al., 2024, Saharia et al., 2022]. These challenges motivate two foundational questions. 

Q1 : Can diffusion models efficiently model manifold data, and how does their sample complexity scale with intrinsic (as opposed to ambient) dimensionality? 

Q2 : How do geometric properties—such as curvature—affect the quality of generative performance? 

1.1 Contribution 

In this paper, we develop a formal statistical framework for diffusion models in the context of data supported on unknown low-dimensional manifolds. We answer the two questions by viewing diffusion models as an unsupervised learner aiming to estimate the underlying data distribution. Different from traditional density estimation methods, diffusion models implicitly learn the dis-tribution by estimating the score function, which presents significant challenges to the analysis. One key issue is that the score function can be highly irregular, even if the manifold and the data distribution are relatively smooth. In particular, it is well-known that the score function has an exploding magnitude as the backward diffusion process approaches zero noise [Song and Ermon, 2019, Kim et al., 2021, Pidstrigach, 2022]. To address this challenge, we propose a novel score decomposition approach, which projects the score according to the noise level, inspired by prior work on modeling data distributions on low-dimensional subspaces [Oko et al., 2023, Chen et al., 2023a]. Our contributions are three-fold: (1) we identify key intrinsic structures of score functions for manifold data under both the large noise (Lemma 3.1) and small noise (Lemma 3.2) regimes; (2) 2based on the structures, we construct suitable neural network architectures that can effectively approximate the score function in diffusion models (Theorem 4.4), where we highlight the influence of the curvature of manifold; and (3) we provide statistical rates for estimating the score function and further the data distribution (Theorems 5.1 and 5.2). The convergence rates depend on the intrinsic dimension of the data as well as the curvature of the manifold. We summarize our main theoretical results in the following theorem. 

Theorem 1.1 (Informal) . Under some manifold assumptions, 1. the score function exhibits distinct decompositions in the large noise and small noise regimes: in the large-noise regime, it decomposes as a weighted sum of localized components that guide noisy data toward the manifold; in the small-noise regime, it decomposes via direct projection onto the manifold, with an additional interaction term that reflects the influence of curvature; 2. based on the decompositions, the score function can be approximated by a ReLU neural network with eO(ϵ−d/β )non-zero weights, where ϵ is the target approximation error. The network size scales expo-nentially only in the intrinsic dimension d, while the dependence on the ambient dimension D

is polynomial with a degree governed by the manifold intrinsic dimension and curvature. 3. furthermore, the data distribution is estimated in terms of Wasserstein distance at a rate 

eO(n− β+1  

> d+2 β

),

where n is the sample size. Our results provide crucial insights into how diffusion models capture low-dimensional data structures. While scaling up models and data sets has driven progress in data distribution model-ing [Kaplan et al., 2020, Henighan et al., 2020], such approaches can be inefficient if they overlook the underlying structure of the data. For instance, Stable Diffusion employs a pre-trained varia-tional autoencoder to first extract key features and reduce dimensionality before applying diffusion, demonstrating the value of architecture adaptation [Esser et al., 2024]. Through our theoretical framework—encompassing approximation and estimation—we emphasize the need to tailor diffu-sion models to unknown low-dimensional structures, thereby tightening approximation and estima-tion bounds. This work paves the way for more efficient architectures and training strategies that explicitly leverage these structures. 

1.2 Related Work 

Recent theoretical studies have framed diffusion models as unsupervised distribution learners and samplers, establishing their statistical distribution learning guarantees [Oko et al., 2023, Chen et al., 2023a, Dou et al., 2024, Mei and Wu, 2025] and sampling convergence guarantees [Block et al., 2020, Chen et al., 2022c, Lee et al., 2023, Chen et al., 2023b, Benton et al., 2023, Li and Yan, 2024, Li et al., 2024]. Such results offer invaluable theoretical insights into the efficiency and accuracy of diffusion models for modeling complex data. Specifically, Oko et al. [2023] analyzes L2-error bounds in score estimation through score matching with neural networks, showing that the learned distribution achieves minimax optimal rates. Chen et al. [2023a] establishes explicit convergence rates when data reside on low-dimensional linear subspaces. Mei and Wu [2025] interprets neural 3networks in diffusion models as denoising mechanisms, enabling an efficient score approximation in graphical models. Moreover, several works have investigated diffusion models when data are supported on man-ifolds. De Bortoli [2022] makes the first attempt to analyze diffusion models for learning low-dimensional manifold data. Assuming an accurate score estimator, De Bortoli [2022] provides distribution estimation guarantees of diffusion models in terms of the Wasserstein distance, though the obtained convergence rate has an exponential dependence on the manifold diameter. Huang et al. [2024] establishes a sampling theory for diffusion models on manifold data, with sampling speed scaling nearly linearly with the intrinsic data dimension. Tang and Yang [2024] proves a convergence rate of the distribution estimator induced by diffusion models only depends on the intrinsic data dimension. However, as Yakovlev and Puchkin [2025] observes, the hidden constants in Tang and Yang [2024]’s convergence rates depend exponentially on the ambient dimension D,which makes the derived rate ineffective when the ambient dimension is of logarithm order of sam-ple size. Azangulov et al. [2024] improves the D-dependence to polynomial order, but without characterizing how convergence rates depend on manifold curvature. Yakovlev and Puchkin [2025] studies denoising scoring matching under relaxed manifold assumptions, considering noisy sam-ples from a single-chart low-dimensional manifold. Additionally, Li and Yan [2024] show that the DDPM sampler adapts to unknown low-dimensional structure with iteration complexity scaling in the intrinsic dimension. Li et al. [2026] reveal a separation between learning the data geometry and the data distribution from a non-statistical learning perspective. They show that recovering the data distribution demands significantly more accurate score function than recovering the data manifold. Farghly et al. [2025] study the influence of manifold curvature on distribution approx-imation via diffusion models, establishing manifold-adaptive rates for a smoothed score function, though without sample complexity bounds. Beyond the manifold setting, ? establish Wasserstein-p

convergence rates for diffusion models that scale with a notion of Wasserstein dimension, a covering-number-based complexity measure generalizing the Minkowski dimension. Our work addresses a fundamental gap in these theoretical analyses by explicitly characterizing how geometric properties of the data manifold—particularly its reach—govern both the efficiency of score estimation and subsequent distribution recovery guaranties. This geometric perspective reveals previously unexplored connections between manifold curvature and the performance of diffusion models.

This section provides a brief introduction to manifolds and diffusion models. Readers may refer to Tu [2010], Flaherty and do Carmo [2013] for a comprehensive introduction to manifolds and Chan [2024], Chen et al. [2024], Tang and Zhao [2024] for a technical exposure of diffusion models. 

2.1 Riemannian Manifold and Density 

We denote M as a d-dimensional Riemannian manifold isometrically embedded in RD with d ≪ D.On a coarse level, a manifold M is a set which is locally Euclidean, meaning that a small local neighborhood on M can be continuously mapped into a Euclidean space. This is formalized with the definition of charts .

Definition 2.1 (Chart) . A chart for M is a pair ( U, ϕ ) such that U ⊂ M is open and ϕ : U 7 → Rd,

where ϕ is a homeomorphism (i.e., bijective, ϕ and ϕ−1 are both continuous). A manifold typically consists of many local neighborhoods overlapping with each other. We say two charts ( U, ϕ ) and ( V, ψ ) on M are C∞ compatible if and only if the transition functions, 

ϕ ◦ ψ−1 : ψ(U ∩ V ) 7 → ϕ(U ∩ V ) and ψ ◦ ϕ−1 : ϕ(U ∩ V ) 7 → ψ(U ∩ V ),

are both C∞.

Definition 2.2 (C∞ Atlas) . A C∞ atlas for M is a collection of pairwise C∞ compatible charts 

{(Uk, ϕ k)}k∈A such that S 

> k∈A

Uk = M.

Definition 2.3 (Smooth Manifold) . A smooth manifold is a manifold together with a C∞ atlas. Classical examples of smooth manifolds are the Euclidean space RD, the torus, and the unit sphere. We further define a Riemannian manifold as a pair ( M, g ), where M is a smooth manifold and g is a Riemannian metric [Lee, 2018, Chapter 2]. An isometric embedding of the d-dimensional 

M in RD is an embedding that preserves the Riemannian metric of M. For more rigorous state-ments, see the classic reference [Flaherty and do Carmo, 2013]. 

2.1.1 Exponential Map 

In this paper, we will frequently work with a common choice of atlas on a Riemannian manifold, constructed from exponential maps. Given a point x ∈ M , we denote TxM ⊂ RD as the tangent space of M at x; a formal definition is provided in [Tu, 2010, Section 8.1]. The tangent space TxM

is a d-dimensional vector space and isomorphic to Rd. Therefore, we can identify vectors in TxM

with their d-dimensional representations, which we denote as the space T dx M ⊂ Rd. We refer to 

TxM and T dx M both as tangent spaces. To define exponential maps, we begin with generalizing straight lines in Euclidean spaces to manifolds. We denote the Riemannian distance dM : M × M → R on M as 

dM(x, y ) = inf {length (γ) | γ is a C1(M) curve such that γ(0) = x, γ (1) = y},

where length (γ) is the length of curve γ computed with respect to the Riemannian metric g. Rie-mannian distance dM(x, y ) is referred to as the length of the shortest path or geodesic connecting 

x and y on the manifold. Exponential map builds upon geodesics. 5Definition 2.4 (Exponential map) . Let x ∈ M . For a tangent vector v ∈ T dx M, there is a unique geodesic γ that starts at x with initial velocity v, i.e., γ(0) = x and γ′(0) = v. The exponential map centered at x is given by Exp x(v) = γ(1), for all v ∈ T dx M.As can be seen, the exponential map takes a vector v ∈ Rd on the tangent space T dx M as input. The output, Exp x(v) ∈ RD, is the point on the manifold obtained by traveling along a (unit speed) geodesic curve that starts at x and has initial direction v; see Figure 1 for an illustration. It is known that for all x ∈ M , there exists a radius r such that the exponential map restricted to a ball BdTxM(0 , r ) is a diffeomorphism onto its image, i.e., it is a smooth map with smooth inverse. As the sufficiently small r-ball in the tangent space may vary for each x ∈ M , we define the injectivity radius of M as the minimum r over all x ∈ M .

Definition 2.5 (Injectivity radius) . For all x ∈ M , the injectivity radius at x is defined as 

inj M(x) = sup {r > 0 | Exp x : BdTxM(0 , r ) 7 → M is a diffeomorphism }. Then the injectivity radius of M is defined as 

inj (M) = inf {inj M(x) | x ∈ M} .<latexit sha1_base64="vtUhjvouiS6k76ttA9H6+OxdfEI=">AAAB6HicbVDLTgJBEOzFF+IL9ehlIjHxRHaNQY9ELx4hkUcCGzI79MLI7OxmZtZICF/gxYPGePWTvPk3DrAHBSvppFLVne6uIBFcG9f9dnJr6xubW/ntws7u3v5B8fCoqeNUMWywWMSqHVCNgktsGG4EthOFNAoEtoLR7cxvPaLSPJb3ZpygH9GB5CFn1Fip/tQrltyyOwdZJV5GSpCh1it+dfsxSyOUhgmqdcdzE+NPqDKcCZwWuqnGhLIRHWDHUkkj1P5kfuiUnFmlT8JY2ZKGzNXfExMaaT2OAtsZUTPUy95M/M/rpCa89idcJqlByRaLwlQQE5PZ16TPFTIjxpZQpri9lbAhVZQZm03BhuAtv7xKmhdlr1Ku1C9L1ZssjjycwCmcgwdXUIU7qEEDGCA8wyu8OQ/Oi/PufCxac042cwx/4Hz+AOnLjQc=</latexit>

Definition 2.6 (Reach [Federer, 1959]) . The reach τ of a manifold M is defined as the quantity 

τ = inf {r > 0 | ∃ x̸ = y ∈ M , v ∈ RD such that r = ∥x − v∥ = ∥y − v∥ = inf 

z∈M ∥z − v∥} .

Intuitively, if the distance of a point x to M is smaller than the reach, then there is a unique point in M that is closest to x. However, if the distance between x and M is larger than the reach, then there will no longer be a unique closest point to x in M. For example, the reach of a sphere is its radius. The reach gives us control over the injectivity radius inj (M). 

Proposition 2.7 (Proof in Aamari and Levrard [2019]) . For a manifold M with reach τ > 0, it holds that inj (M) ≥ πτ .

Remark 2.8 (Atlas using exponential map) . Constructing an atlas on a compact Riemannian manifold M using exponential maps is commonly adopted. In particular, recall that for any point 

x ∈ M , the exponential map is diffeomorphic on a ball BdTxM(0 , r ) with r < inj (M). Denote 

Ux = Exp x(BdTxM(0 , r )) as a local neighborhood, whose union ∪x∈M Ux forms an open cover of M.For a compact M, there exists a finite sub-cover {Uxk }CM

k=1 . As shown in Chen et al. [2022a], CM

depends on the reach and dimension of the manifold. We suppress the subscript xk to k and thus, 

{(Uk, Log k)}CM

k=1 becomes an atlas on M.

2.1.2 Differentiable Function on Manifold 

The existence of an atlas allows us to define differentiable functions on M.6Definition 2.9 (Cs Functions on M). Let M be a d-dimensional Riemannian manifold isomet-rically embedded in RD. A function f : M 7 → R is Cs if for any chart ( U, ϕ ), the composition 

f ◦ ϕ−1 : ϕ(U ) 7 → R is continuously differentiable up to order s.We note that the definition of Cs functions is independent of the choice of the chart ( U, ϕ ). Suppose ( V, ψ ) is another chart and V ∩ U̸ = ∅. Then we have f ◦ ψ−1 = ( f ◦ ϕ−1) ◦ (ϕ ◦ ψ−1). Since 

M is a smooth manifold, ( U, ϕ ) and ( V, ψ ) are C∞ compatible. Thus, f ◦ ϕ−1 is Cs and ϕ ◦ ψ−1

is C∞, and their composition is Cs. The following definition generalizes Cs functions to H¨ older continuous functions.

We next introduce the partition of unity, which plays a crucial role in our construction of neural networks. 

Definition 2.11 (Partition of Unity, Definition 13.4 in Tu [2010]) . A C∞ partition of unity on a manifold M is a collection of nonnegative C∞ functions ρk : M 7 → R+ for k ≥ 1 such that 1. the collection of supports, {supp( ρk)}k∈A , is locally finite, i.e., every point on M has a neighborhood that meets only finitely many of supp( ρk)’s; 2. ρk = 1. For a smooth manifold, a C∞ partition of unity always exists. 

Proposition 2.12 (Existence of a C∞ partition of unity, Theorem 13.7 in Tu [2010]) . Let {Uk}k∈A 

be an open cover of a compact smooth manifold M. Then there is a C∞ partition of unity {ρk}k∈A 

where every ρk has a compact support such that supp( ρk) ⊂ Uk.

2.1.3 Distribution and Density on Manifold 

To define distributions and density functions on M, we first define the volume measure on a manifold M and establish integration. 

Definition 2.13 (Volume measure) . For a compact d-dimensional Riemannian manifold M, its volume measure μM is the d-dimensional Hausdorff measure on M [Federer, 1959]. 7We say that a data distribution Pdata supported on M has a density pdata if the Radon-Nikodym derivative of Pdata with respect to μM is pdata . According to Evans and Gariepy [1992], for any continuous function f : M 7 → R supported within the image of the ball BdTxM(0 , r ) under the exponential map for r < inj (M), we have

For convenience, we denote the normalized version of Pdata ,k as μk, which is a probability distribution supported on Uk. Then it holds that Pdata = P 

> k

Pdata ,k (Uk) · μk, where Pdata ,k (Uk)is the total mass of μk. Such a decomposition into local measures is crucial to our analysis on structures in the score function in Section 3. 

2.2 Diffusion Model 

We adopt a continuous-time description of diffusion models for the ease of theoretical analysis. There are two coupled processes in diffusion models. The forward process progressively injects in-dependent Gaussian noise to clean data. Following the convention in literature, we use an Ornstein-Ulhenbeck process (also known as the variance preserving process): dXt = − 12 Xtdt + d Bt with X0 ∼ Pdata , (2.3) where ( Bt)t≥0 is a Wiener process. We denote the marginal distribution of Xt at time t as Pt.Under (2.3), given X0, the conditional distribution of Xt|X0 is Gaussian N(αtX0, h tID), where 

αt = exp( −t/ 2) and ht = 1 − α2 

> t

. Consequently, under mild conditions, (2.3) transforms the initial distribution Pdata to P∞ = N(0 , I D) at infinite time. In practice, the forward process (2.3) will terminate at a sufficiently large time T > 0, where the corrupted marginal distribution PT is expected to be close to the standard Gaussian distribution. When generating new samples, diffusion models reverse the time of (2.3), which leads to the following backward process, dYt =

 12 Yt + ∇ log pT −t(Yt)



dt + d Bt with Y0 ∼ PT , (2.4) 8where ∇ log pt(·) is the so-called score function, i.e., the gradient of log probability density function of Pt, and Bt is another independent Wiener process. With mild assumptions, the backward process (Yt)0≤t≤T has the same distribution as the time-reversed version of the forward process ( XT −t)0≤t≤T

[Anderson, 1982, Haussmann and Pardoux, 1986]. Simulating (2.4), however, poses substantial difficulties, since both the score function ∇ log pt

and initial distribution PT are unknown. To address these challenges, we replace PT by the standard Gaussian distribution. Moreover, we use a score estimator bs instead of the ground truth score 

∇ log pt. The estimated score bs is parameterized by a neural network. With these substitutions, we obtain the following backward SDE, d bYt =

 12 bYt + bs( bYt, T − t)



dt + d Bt with bY0 ∼ N(0 , I D). (2.5) In practice, new data are generated by applying a discretization scheme to simulate (2.5). The estimated score bs is obtained by score matching [Song and Ermon, 2019, Ho et al., 2020], which solves a least square problem aggregated over time t ≤ T . Conceptually, we minimize min

where F is a class of score functions and t0 is an early-stopping time for stabilizing the training [Song and Ermon, 2019, 2020]. Since ∇ log pt is not tractable with unknown Pdata , loss R cannot be directly implemented and optimized. Thanks to the seminal work [Hyv¨ arinen and Dayan, 2005], an equivalent loss (up to a constant) is derived: min

3 Structure in Score Function for Manifold Data 

The score function ∇ log pt is the steering power of diffusion models in generating high-fidelity samples. In this section, we study structures in the score function, when the ground-truth data distribution Pdata is supported on a d-dimensional Riemannian manifold M. We present key insights on how the original manifold structure and data distribution are progressively restored from pure noise in the backward dynamics, driven by the score function. Formally, we show that the score function decomposes distinctively at different stages of the backward process. These properties inform an efficient representation and estimation of the score function. 93.1 Warmup: Linear Subspace Data 

We start with a simple yet intriguing example—data concentrated on a linear subspace. Consider data x ∈ RD being represented as x = Az , where A ∈ RD×d is an unknown matrix with orthonormal columns and z ∈ Rd is a latent variable following some underlying distribution. This says that each data point lies in the subspace spanned by the column vectors of matrix A. For any t > 0, the score function decomposes into two orthogonal components as 

∇ log pt(x) = αtE[X0|Xt = x] − ΠA(x)

ht

| {z }

> sA(x,t ): on-support score

− x − ΠA(x)

ht

| {z }

> s⊥(x,t ): orthogonal score

, (3.1) where Π A(x) = AA ⊤x is the projection onto the column span of matrix A. The same result is presented in Chen et al. [2023a] with an explicit formula for the conditional expectation E[X0|Xt =

x] using the latent variable. We term the two components as the on-support score sA and the orthogonal score s⊥, respectively. The on-support score sA lies in the column space of matrix A

as E[X0|Xt = x] concentrated on the subspace. The orthogonal score function s⊥(x, t ) = − 1 

> ht

(I −

AA ⊤)x is perpendicular to the subspace. As time t → 0, the variance ht of the added noise tends to zero, and we check that s⊥ blows up. This is consistent with the score blowup issue for learning generic structured data Song and Ermon [2019], Kim et al. [2021], Pidstrigach [2022]. The score decomposition suggests decoupled dynamics in the backward process on the subspace and its orthogonal complement. The on-support score sA aims to recover the distribution of the latent variable, while the orthogonal score s⊥ dictates a contraction towards the subspace whenever the noisy state x is not on the subspace. As t → 0, the strength of the contraction to the subspace amplifies and finally pushes data onto the subspace in the limit. Structures in the score function motivate an efficient statistical argument to demystify the empirical performance of diffusion models in complex high-dimensional data Oko et al. [2023], Chen et al. [2023a]. However, linear subspace is oversimplified for real-world applications, spelling the need for a generalization to generic geometric data, which we present in the next section. 

3.2 Nonlinear Manifold Data 

We generalize the decomposition in Section 3.1 to compact d-dimensional Riemannian manifolds. On the one hand, the score function for a manifold M presents local structures analogous to that for the linear subspace case, given that manifolds locally resemble Euclidean space. On the other hand, the score function possesses time-inhomogeneous behavior, and the score decomposition becomes more involved due to nonlinearity. We investigate structures of the score function in two stages depending on the amount of noise injected in data. We refer to “ large noise ” when t is large in the forward process (2.3), and “ small noise ” when t is small. The forward process suppresses the data magnitude exponentially fast, i.e., 

αt = e−t/ 2, therefore, in the large noise regime, injected noise dominates the corrupted data. The behavior of the score function centers around pushing noisy data towards a close vicinity of the original data manifold. On the other hand, in the small noise regime, the score function recovers geometric details of the manifold and eventually recovers the clean data distribution. Figure 2 demonstrates the distinct behaviors in two regimes. A two-time-scale perspective also appears in Dou et al. [2024]. We formalize our analysis in the sequel. 10 3.2.1 Large Noise: Weighted Decomposition 

Given a large time t, due to the dominance of the injected noise, it suffices to consider a coarse linear approximation to the manifold M constructed by the tangent spaces at xk for k = 1 , . . . , C M.For the k-th chart, we denote the projection of x onto the shrunk tangent space αt · Txk M as Πk(x, t ) = argmin  

> y∈αt·TxkM

∥y − x∥ for k = 1 , . . . , C M.

We recall that αt is the shrinkage ratio introduced by the forward process (2.3) at time t. Based on projections onto tangent spaces and the decomposition of manifold distribution into a mixture of local measures in Remark 2.14, we present the following weighted score decomposition, whose proof is provided in Appendix A.1. 

Lemma 3.1. For a large time t > 0 and an arbitrary noisy state x ∈ RD, the score function 

∇ log pt(x) takes the form:

Decomposition on Single Chart For the k-th chart, sk 

> ⊥

acts analogously to the orthogonal score in the linear subspace case. While the difference is that Π k(x, t ) is a time-dependent projection onto the rescaled tangent space. As a result, the noisy state x moves towards the tangent space

Understanding Weight wk Recall the decomposition of Pdata into local measures on each chart. The weight wk is interpreted as the probability that a given noisy state x eventually falls into the k-th chart. If x is close to the scaled chart αtUk, the score function is dominated by the corresponding chart. That is, wk ≈ 1 and the score function behaves as if there is only one chart on the manifold. On the contrary, when x is not close to any of αtUk, the score function behaves as that of a mixture of distributions. Attentive readers may find wk echos that in Gaussian mixture models. In fact, for a Gaussian mixture PCM

Our localized measure Pdata ,k generalizes the prior probability. 11 Anticipated Mean EX0∼μk [X0|Xt = x] The anticipated mean can be interpreted as a posterior average, where the prior distribution is μk and the current observation is Xt = x. Equivalently, we presume that the noisy state generates clean data belonging to Uk. Unfortunately, the anticipated mean is generally not on the manifold or the tangent space due to nonlinearity, leaving sk

3.2.2 Small Noise: Local Decomposition 

Driven by the score function in the large noise regime, noisy states gradually reduce noise and evolve towards a close vicinity of the manifold. Then the small noise regime emerges, since the coarse piecewise linear approximation using tangent spaces becomes insufficient for precise manifold recovery. Instead, the score function operates with higher precision, accurately capturing the local geometry of M around x through orthogonal projection directly onto M.Suppose M has a positive reach τ . We define an inflated region around M as K(M, τ ) = S 

> x∈M

B(x, τ ). At time t, for any x ∈ αtK(M, τ ), we define ΠM(x, t ) = argmin 

> y∈αtM

∥y − x∥. (3.2) The projection (3.2) is well-defined as shown in Leobacher and Steinicke [2020]. The following decomposition of the score function is established using the projection operator, whose proof is provided in Appendix A.2. 

Lemma 3.2. For any fixed time t > 0, given an arbitrary x ∈ αtK(M, τ ), the score function 

∇ log pt(x) decomposes into 

∇ log pt(x) = αtE[X0|Xt = x] − ΠM(x, t )

> sM: on-support score

− x − ΠM(x, t )

Compared to the large noise regime in Lemma 3.1, small noise decomposition does not assert a mixed structure. Alternatively, the decomposition is driven by the unique projection Π M(x, t )onto the manifold, resembling that in the linear subspace case. The reason behind this is that the variance of the added noise to state x is small. It is less likely that x is obtained by corrupting distant clean data points on the manifold. We provide the following further discussions. 

Hitting αtK(M, τ ) with High Probability Conditioned on a clean data point x0 ∈ M , the distribution of Xt is Gaussian N(αtx0, h tI). When t is sufficiently small, it holds that αt ≈ 1 and 

ht ≈ 0. Therefore, we have √Dh t < α tτ , meaning that ∥Xt − αtx0∥ < α tτ with high probability. Consequently, αtK(M, τ ) is hit by noisy states with high probability in the forward process (2.3). More importantly, when τ is small, K(M, τ ) is a narrow band around the manifold, making the requirement on small t stringent. As a result, the learning of the score function becomes challenging. We present explicit approximation and estimation complexities of the score function in relation to the reach τ in Section 4. 12 Further Decomposition in sM While Lemma 3.2 replicates the same form of score decomposi-tion as the linear subspace, the on-support score possesses intricate structures captured by E1 and 

E2. Term E1 attempts to distribute the probability mass at the projection point to the manifold according to the Gaussian transition kernel defined in (2.3). Unfortunately, the nonlinear manifold introduces a nuisance: the movement x − ΠM(x, t ) is not orthogonal to the movement towards the projection Π M(x, t ) − αtx0. As a result, E2 captures the interaction between the two movements, and we cannot decouple the dynamics in the backward process as in the linear subspace case. It is worth mentioning that the interaction term E2 heavily depends on the curvature of the manifold. As a sanity check, when the manifold reduces to a linear subspace without curvature, E2 vanishes and Lemma 3.2 reduces to (3.1). Figure 2 illustrates such an interaction. 

Implication of Score Decomposition The score decompositions in Lemmas 3.1 and 3.2 are key to an efficient neural network approximation developed in Section 4. The on-support scores map 

D-dimensional noisy states to points that can be effectively approximated using d-dimensional representations, since the manifold assumes low-dimensional local charts. !A(x2)

Figure 2: Score decomposition for linear subspace and general manifold. For linear subspace data, the projection Π A is globally defined for all t and noisy state x. The score function decomposes into two orthogonal components. For a smooth manifold, the score function behaves distinctly according to the magnitude of the added noise. In the large noise regime, score function decomposes corresponding to tangent spaces on the manifold. In the small noise regime, local decomposition exists and is centered around the projection point Π M(xt2 , t 2) of a noisy state xt2 .

4 Approximating Score Function using Neural Network 

In practice, score functions are parameterized by deep neural networks. Leveraging the structures in score functions identified in Section 3, we develop score approximation theories. We impose some mild regularity conditions on the manifold. 

Assumption 4.1 (Data Domain). M is a d-dimensional compact Riemannian manifold isometri-cally embedded in RD. There exists a constant BM > 0 such that, for any point x ∈ M , it holds that ∥x∥∞ ≤ BM. The reach of M is lower bounded by τ > 0. A positive reach of M ensures geometric regularity by ruling out sharp corners, cusps, or self-intersections. This enables well-defined projections onto the manifold, and ensures that local charts behave nicely with bounded distortion and controlled curvature. Many commonly studied manifolds satisfy such regularity conditions, including the unit sphere Sd and the torus Td.13 Assumption 4.2 . For the atlas in Remark 2.8, there exists a constant η ∈ (0 , 1] such that choosing 

r = 3 τ ensures B(xk, ητ ) ∩ M is a subset of Uk.Assumption 4.2 is an implicit regularity condition for the geometric structure of M. While exponential map Exp x is well-defined on BTxM(0 , 3τ ) by Proposition 2.7, Assumption 4.2 further guarantees its inverse map Log x is well-defined so that Exp x is locally a bijection. In addition, we assume the smoothness of the ground-truth probability density pdata .

Assumption 4.3 (Data Distribution). The data distribution Pdata has a density pdata supported on M and belonging to Hβ (M) for a H¨ older index β > 0. Moreover, there exists a constant Cf > 1such that C−1 

> f

≤ pdata (x) ≤ Cf for any x ∈ M .Assumption 4.3 generalizes the standard H¨ older densities in nonparametric statistics Tsybakov [2008], Wasserman [2006] to Riemannian manifolds. We require the density bounded below by a positive constant for technical convenience, which is often adopted in the existing literature Tsybakov [2008], Oko et al. [2023]. We parameterize the score function using a class of deep neural networks F with entrywise ReLU activation (i.e., ReLU( a) = max {a, 0}): 

F(L, W, S, B, R ) := f (x) = AL · ReLU( · · · ReLU( A1x + b1) · · · ) + bL :width bounded by W,

Hyper-parameters control the depth ( L) and width ( W ) of the network, as well as the sparsity ( S)and boundedness ( B and R). The following theorem shows that the network class F can accurately represent the score function. 

Theorem 4.4. [Score Approximation Theory] Suppose Assumptions 4.1-4.3 hold. For any suffi-ciently small ϵ > 0 and any time t ∈ [t0, T ] with t0 = poly( n−1) and T = O(log(1 /ϵ )), there exists a network class F(L, W, S, B, R ) that yields a function ¯ s satisfying

The complete proof is provided in Appendix B, and a proof sketch is presented in Section 4.2. The precise requirement on ϵ is specified in condition (B.39). Theorem 4.4 establishes an L2 score approximation guarantee over the unbounded domain RD, while existing universal approximation theory of neural networks majorly focuses on approximating target functions on a compact domain under the L∞ norm Yarotsky [2017], Schmidt-Hieber [2020], Chen et al. [2022a]. Our analysis addresses the unboundedness through a truncation argument. Moreover, to achieve an ϵ approxi-mation error, the network size scales exponentially with the intrinsic dimension d, emphasizing the adaptability of neural networks to the manifold structures. Several additional remarks are in turn. 14 Influence of Manifold Curvature Theorem 4.4 suggests that the approximation error needs to scale with the reach τ to capture manifold structures. A slight rewrite of the error guarantee shows an explicit impact of the reach. For any ϵ ∈ (0 , 1) and γ > 0, the same network class F in Theorem 4.4 gives rise to a ¯ s with 

∥¯s(x, t ) − ∇ log pt(x)∥2 

> L2(Pt)

= eO D2γ+d+2

τ 2γ + ϵ2

!! 

. (4.2) Setting τ −2γ ϵ2γ/β = ϵ2 recovers the bound in Theorem 4.4. As evident from (4.2), a smaller τ ,corresponding to higher manifold curvature, leads to increased approximation error. The reason behind this is that a manifold with a small τ exhibits rapidly changing local geometry, causing local projections to possess significantly different orientations. These variations are difficult to approximate, especially in the small noise regime. Indeed, E2 in Lemma 3.2 varies significantly as noisy state x moves. 

Dependence on Ambient Dimension The approximation guarantee depends on the ambient dimension D and is determined by the exponent γ. When the reach τ ≥ 1, we can show that γ is upper bounded by β. This indicates that for relatively smoothly varying manifolds, the dependence on the ambient dimension scales at most D2β+d+2 —a polynomial dependence. On the other hand, when τ < 1, γ grows as τ decreases, potentially leading to an exponential dependence on D for extremely small reach. Our bound improves the ambient dimension dependence in Tang and Yang [2024] and explicitly characterizes the interplay between approximation error and the manifold curvature. 

4.1 Large and Small Noise Analysis 

Theorem 4.4 builds upon separate analyses for large noise and small noise regimes. To construct the network class F, we first construct sub-networks for the large noise and small noise regimes respectively, and then incorporate them by a time switching network. The network architecture is depicted in Figure 3. We highlight the respective approximation guarantees in each regime. 

Large Noise Inspired by Lemma 3.1, we construct a network consisting of parallel structures for representing the decomposition in each chart. We summarize the result in the following lemma, whenever t ≥ tlarge , a threshold for the large noise regime.

tFigure 3: Illustration of the neural network architecture. A time switching network aggregates sub-networks for small noise and large noise respectively. Both regimes involve Taylor approximation of on-support scores, and projection approximation as part of orthogonal scores. These approximators are implemented by neural networks. The proof is provided in Appendix B.2. We observe that the network size scales with the intrinsic dimension d. Moreover, the approximation error depends on the curvature of the manifold. More specifically, a smaller reach implies more rapidly varying local geometry, which increases the approximation error through the curvature-dependent factor ( ϵ2/β /τ 2)γ0 . In addition, the network architecture comprises parallel sub-networks corresponding to individual charts, and a smaller reach necessitates finer local approximations that collectively enlarge the network. Compared to the approximate rate in Theorem 4.4, we note that the large noise regime contributes a minor error term, while the dominating error owes to the small noise regime. 

Small Noise Similar to the large noise regime, we identify a threshold tsmall for the emergence of the small noise regime. The approximation guarantee is presented in the following lemma. 

Lemma 4.6 (Small noise score approximation) . Suppose Assumptions 4.1-4.3 hold. 16 The proof is provided in Appendix B.1. Lemma 4.6 guarantees that we can find a network class with size depending on the intrinsic dimension d to efficiently approximate the score function in the small noise regime. Moreover, both the time threshold tsmall and the approximation error depend on the manifold curvature. Specifically, a small reach indicates rapidly changing local geometry, which substantially complicates the approximation of the interaction term E2 in Lemma 3.2, leading to more stringent requirements on the time t and greater representation difficulty. When ϵ is sufficiently small, the time threshold becomes tsmall = log 11−ϵ2/β , dominated by the approximation error. Compared to the large noise regime, with a comparable number of nonzero parameters, neural network approximation suffers from an enlarged error. This is due to the finer approximation to the manifold in the small noise regime, instead of using coarse piecewise linear approximations based on tangent spaces. Consequently, when combining the large and small noise regimes, the small noise error is dominating. The large and small noise regimes overlap in Lemmas 4.5 and 4.6 with tlarge < t small , as shown in Figure 3. This indicates that when t ∈ (tlarge , t small ), either of the score decompositions can be effectively approximated by neural networks. More importantly, the overlap introduces a “grace period” when switching from the large noise regime to the small noise one, where we linearly interpolate between the two regimes. This can be exactly realized by ReLU networks; see details in Section 4.2. 

4.2 Proof Sketch of Theorem 4.4 

The complete proof of Theorem 4.4 is provided in Appendix B, which boils down to developing neural network approximation theories for the large and small noise regimes, respectively. For either the large or small noise regimes, the proof is constructive, which consists of two major components: Step 1. Local polynomial approximation to the on-support and orthogonal scores characterized in Section 3, and Step 2. Implementing local polynomials by neural networks . After approximating the large and small noise scores separately, we complete the proof by Step 3. Introducing a time-switching network to actively select large and small noise score approximations. We focus on the more challenging small noise regime to showcase Steps 1 and 2. The large noise regime replicates similar arguments. Recall from Lemma 3.2 that the on-support score sM

(4.6) Compared to (4.3), we have effectively reduced the integration domain and the number of local charts in (4.6). The remaining arguments construct polynomials for representing the right-hand side of (4.6). While the detailed construction is rather technical, we highlight essential building components. 1. Local density-related function Fk. We show that Fk is β-H¨ older smooth, inheriting the regularity of the data distribution. Therefore, we use local Taylor polynomials of degree at most ⌊β⌋ for approximating Fk in Lemma D.8; 18 2. Exponential function exp( ·). Both E1 and E2 are plugged into an exponential function, which is C∞. We use a Taylor polynomial of degree O(log(1 /ϵ )) for approximating the exponential function of E1. A different approach is adopted for E2, where we consider a Taylor polynomial of degree γ. Note that E2 involves an inner product. When computing powers of E2 in the Taylor polynomial, it is convenient to use tensor inner products. Indeed, for any j ≤ γ, we have [ E2(t)] j = ⟨[x − ΠM(x, t )] ⊗j , [Π M(x, t ) − αtExp k(v)] ⊗j ⟩. Due to the linearity of the tensor inner product, we can extract common factors when summing over 

k ∈ I (x). Detailed construction of these polynomials are provided in Lemma D.9. 3. Exponential map Exp k. The exponential map Exp k is also C∞ on each chart. Different from the previous two components, we use average Taylor polynomials (Brenner [2008, Chap-ter 4.1], see also Definition F.1) for approximation as detailed in Lemmas D.11 and D.13. The average Taylor polynomial provides simultaneous accurate approximation to Exp k and its first-order derivatives. This is critical to ensure that the approximation error of E1 being additionally proportional to ht, leading to a tight approximation error to s1 when t is small. Substituting the building components into (4.6), we obtain a polynomial approximation to the integrand, which depends on the noisy state x, the projection Π M(x, t ), and vector v. Integrating against v over the Euclidean ball Ck yields another polynomial depending on x, Π M(x, t ), and center point vk(x, t ), which approximates s1. Note that the integrating tensors in E2 over Ck leads to tensors with polynomial entries. We denote them as Tensor-Poly. The following lemma presents the formal statement.

The primary challenge lies in approximating vk(x, t ) defined in (4.5). The following proposition proves that vk(x, t ) is C∞ and thereby can also be efficiently approximated by a network Nvk (x, t ) as shown in Lemma D.14. 

Proposition 4.8. Fixing any time t > 0, the function vk(·, t ) : RD → Rd is C∞ on αtK(M, τ ) for every x ∈ αtK(M, τ ) and every k ∈ I (x). 

Proof. By Lemma D.4, function vk(x, t ) is well-defined for x ∈ αtK(M, τ ). According to Leobacher and Steinicke [2020], Π M(·, t )/α t is a C∞ function on αtK(M, τ ), given that M is a smooth manifold. By the definition of atlas in Definition 2.2, the log map Log k is C∞ as well. Therefore, 

vk(·, t ) is C∞ as the composition of two C∞ functions. Now we construct three subnetworks based on the elementary network Nvk (x, t ). 1. Projection network Nproj . As shown in Proposition 4.8, the projection Π M(x, t ) is a C∞-function of x ∈ αtK(M, τ ). Thus each entry of [ x−ΠM(x, t )] ⊗j is C∞, which is approximated by Nj 

> proj

entrywise (Lemma D.18). 2. Chart determination network Ndet . We identify relevant charts by evaluating the Eu-clidean distance ∥x − xk∥ between the noisy state and the center of each chart. Therefore, we use a neural network to approximate an indicator function 1{∥ x − xk∥2 ≤ ¯r(t)2} with ¯ r(t)given in (4.4). This is plausible since the squared Euclidean distance can be well approximated by neural networks. See full details in Lemma D.19. 3. Tensor-Poly network Npoly . We implement each entry in Tensor-Poly k,j by a neural net-work. Aggregating these entries yields Nk,j

(Lemma D.20). Network ¯ s1 in (4.7) approximates s1. Similarly, we construct a network ¯ s2 to approximate s2.Then we have ¯ sM = ¯ s2(x, t )/(√ht ¯s1(x, t )) as the approximation to the on-support score, where the division is realized by an extra network (Lemma B.3. Combining ¯ sM with an additive projection network yields the score estimator ¯ ssmall in the small noise regime. We repeat Steps 1 and 2 to construct ¯ slarge for the large noise regime in Appendix B.2. 

• Step 3. Constructing a time-switching network. To incorporate the large and small noise approximation ¯ ssmall and ¯ slarge into a single score network, we implement a time-switching network consisting of two simple ReLU functions. For any t ∈ [t0, T ], we define two switching function as SW small (t) = 1

tsmall − tlarge 

ReLU  (tsmall − tlarge ) − ReLU( t − tlarge ) + ReLU( t − tsmall ),

SW large (t) = 1

tsmall − tlarge 

ReLU  ReLU( t − tlarge ) − ReLU( t − tsmall ).

Recall that tsmall and tlarge are the thresholds for the large and small noise regimes. The shape of the switching functions is depicted in Figure 3. Using the switching functions, we construct the score network as 

es(x, t ) = SW small (t) · ¯ssmall (x, t ) + SW large (t) · ¯slarge (x, t ).

It can be seen that SW small (t) + SW large (t) = 1 for any t ∈ [t0, T ]. Outside the overlapping interval [ tlarge , t small ], one of the switching functions is identically zero. Only within the overlapping interval, does es(x, t ) become a convex combination of the large and small noise score networks. It is straightforward to implement the multiplication operation in es. This completes the construction of a neural network that approximates the ground-truth score function. 20 5 Sample Complexity of Score and Distribution Estimation 

In this section, we provide sample complexity for score function estimation using the network class 

F, and further establish distribution estimation guarantees using the estimated score function. 

5.1 Score Estimation 

Recall that we denote bs as the empirical risk minimizer of denoising score matching in (2.6). By choosing the score network F as in Theorem 4.4, the following theorem establishes the generalization property of bs.

Theorem 5.1. [Score estimation error bound] Suppose Assumptions 4.1-4.3 hold. Let early-stopping time t0 = n−c with some constant c > 0 and terminal time T = O(log n). We choose the network class F = F(L, W, S, B, R ) as in Theorem 4.4 with ϵ = n−β/ (d+2 β), and R = O  (nc log n)1/2.For a sufficiently large n > τ −(d+2 β), it holds that 1

T − t0

Ex0

Z Tt0

∥bs(x, t ) − ∇ log pt(x)∥2 

> L2(pt)

dt



≲ D2γ+d+2 n− 2βd+2 β ,

where γ = ⌈β(1 + log( τ d+2 β )/ log n)−1⌉.The proof is provided in Appendix C.1. Theorem 5.1 demonstrates the efficiency of neural networks in learning the score function and their adaptivity to the low-dimensional data structure. We provide two remarks. 

Dependence on Dimension The estimation error of bs converges at a rate n− 2βd+2 β , depending exponentially only on the intrinsic dimension d and polynomially on the ambient dimension D.Familiar readers may find that this estimation error rate matches the mimimax rate of density esti-mation for β-H¨ older densities [Tsybakov, 2008]. An intriguing fact about score function estimation is that it inherits the regularity of the clean data distribution, as the score function itself does not directly verify regularity conditions. 

Sample Size Depending on Curvature The requirement on the sample size n > τ −(d+2 β) is nontrivial when the reach is small, i.e., τ < 1. In the case of τ ≥ 1, Theorem 5.1 holds for any sample size n. Consistent with previous discussions, a small τ induces complicated local structures on the manifold, making it fundamentally challenging for learning. Therefore, the sample size needs to scale with the reach to ensure a plausible estimation. 

5.2 Distribution Estimation 

We transfer the score estimation guarantee to distribution estimation guarantee, since the backward process (2.5) is solely driven by the learned score function: d bYt =

The proof is given in Appendix C.2. The distribution recovery rate matches the minimax optimal rate for learning a β-H¨ older distribution supported on [0 , 1] d, which is a special case of 

d-dimensional manifold. 

Proposition 5.3 (Theorem 3 in Niles-Weed and Berthet [2022]) . Let Pβd be a class of distributions satisfying Assumptions 4.1 and 4.3 with M = [0 , 1] d. Then for any β > 0 and d ≥ 2, we have inf

≳ n− β+1  

> d+2 β

where the infimum is taken over all estimators bPn based on n observations. Our theory shows that neural networks efficiently learn the score function, which in turn enables optimal distribution recovery. It validates that the generated samples will faithfully reflect both the geometric structure of the manifold and the distribution of the original data. 

6 Conclusion 

In this paper, we have provided a theoretical framework for understanding diffusion models for low-dimensional data. By analyzing the score function’s structure and its dependence on the curvature, we have derived approximation and estimation guarantees. Our results demonstrate that diffusion models circumvent the curse of dimensionality by adapting to the underlying structures of the data, with convergence rates governed by intrinsic dimension and manifold curvature. These insights not only explain why diffusion models excel in practice, but also highlight the importance of tailoring architectures to data structure.

B Proofs in Section 4 

With the goal of proving Theorem 4.4, we present the analysis for small noise first in Appendix B.1 and large noise in Appendix B.2. In Appendix B.3, we combine the results in large and small noise regimes to complete the proof of Theorem 4.4. To ease the presentation, we frequently defer technical lemmas to Appendix D. 

B.1 Small Noise: Proof of Lemma 4.6 

The small noise regime is determined by the following conditions on time t,

The conditions in (B.1) identify the small time regime where the variance ht of the added noise is smaller than the target accuracy ϵ, while simultaneously ensuring that the projections of input points onto the manifold are well-defined. Notably, in the limit of small approximation error ϵ → 0, the constraints in (B.1) are dominated by the condition ht ≤ ϵ2/β . Conditions in (B.1) imply the following two inequalities: 2pDh t log(1 /ϵ ) ≤ ηα t

Our approach involves a dual treatment of ¯ ssmall depending on the spatial domain: within the region Kt(δ), we design ¯ ssmall to achieve pointwise approximation in terms of the L∞ norm, while outside this region, we only require ¯ ssmall to remain bounded. • 1. Local density-related function Fk. Recall that Fk(v) = ( ρk · pdata ) ◦ Exp k(v) · Gk(v) for 

v ∈ B d(0 , r ). We claim that Fk is a non-negative β-H¨ older function on Bd(0 , r ) with a bounded H¨ older norm CF > 0. To see this, we observe that pdata ∈ H β (M), while the partition of unity ρk,the exponential map Exp k, and the square root of the determinant of the Jacobian matrix Gk are all 

C∞. It is clear that CF depends on the bounds on the derivatives of ρk, Exp k, and Gk. Accordingly, we adopt Taylor polynomials of degree ⌊β⌋ to approximate Fk. Let θ = [ θ1, . . . , θ d]⊤ ∈ Nd be a multi-index, and we define

B.1.2 Neural Network Implementation 

We construct our neural network based on implementing function f5 in (B.16). We start with developing two elementary networks: 1) Nvk approximates the low-dimensional representation vk,and 2) N× realizes multiplication. We then construct three key networks: 1) projection network 

Nproj , 2) chart determination network Nchart , and 3) Tensor-Poly network Npoly to approximate key components in f5. Accordingly, the constructed network takes the form: ¯s1(x, t ) =

Elementary Network Construction . We first approximate the low-dimensional representation 

vk(x, t ) of a noisy state x at time t. By definition, vk(x, t ) projects x onto the shrunk manifold 

αtM first, then maps the projection to the low-dimensional space Rd. Thanks to Proposition 4.8, 

vk is C∞ in x and thereby can be efficiently approximated by a network. By Lemma D.14, for any approximation error ϵv ∈ (0 , 1), there exists a network Nvk = [ Nvk ,1, Nvk ,2, . . . , Nvk ,d ]⊤ satisfying sup

• 2. Chart determination network Ndet . The chart determination network Ndet identifies the index set I(x) = {k : ∥x − xk∥ ≤ ¯r(t)}. For an index k ∈ I (x), it must verify the condition 

1{∥ x−xk∥2 ≤ ¯r(t)2} = 1. As a result, Ndet implements the indicator function by (i) approximating the squared Euclidean distance using a network bd, and (ii) implementing the indicator function using another network b1. The composition Ndet = b1 ◦ bd yields the desired approximation to the index set. The detailed network construction and approximation guarantee are provided in Lemma D.19. Given ϵdet ∈ (0 , 1), x ∈ K t(ϵ), and any k ∈ [CM], it holds that

41 B.2 Large Noise: Proof of Lemma 4.5 

Score approximation is less challenging in the large noise regime, where clean data is corrupted by Gaussian noise with large variance ht. The resulting score function is smoother than the small noise counterpart. The large noise regime is characterized by 

ht ≥ ϵ2/β /4, (B.28) where ϵ > 0 is the approximation error we aim to achieve. We will choose ϵ later so that the large noise and small noise regimes overlap. Our goal is to construct a network ¯ slarge (x, t ) approximating the score function ∇ log pt(x) in this regime. By the same truncation argument in (B.3), it suffices to construct ¯ slarge (x, t ) for a pointwise approximation to ∇ log pt for any x ∈ K t(ϵ). In this regime, the precise projection Π M(x, t ) is no longer necessary. Instead, it suffices to decompose the score into components with respect to each local tangent space of M and orthogonal components perpendicular to these tangent spaces. The tangent space projections Π k have explicit forms of linear operations and thus avoid the complex approximation of Π M. Lemma 3.1 provides the following score decomposition:

B.3 Proof of Theorem 4.4 

We prove Theorem 4.4 by constructing a single network that well approximates the score function 

∇ log pt(x) for any time t ∈ [t0, T ]. First, we impose the following conditions on the approximation error ϵ > 0 so that the small noise and large noise regimes overlap: 

ϵ ≤ min 

( √ητ rD 1/4√B , η min {τ, √τ }

16 max {√D, 4LExp LLog } , 1

ht ≤ ϵ2/β . Therefore, given tsmall = log 11−ϵ2/β and tlarge = log 11−ϵ2/β /4 , the small noise regime is exactly [ t0, t small ], which overlaps with the large noise regime [ tlarge , T ] in [ tlarge , t small ]. Next, we construct the time switching network to incorporate networks ¯ ssmall in Lemma 4.6 and ¯slarge in Lemma 4.5. For any t ∈ [t0, T ], we define two switching function as SW small (t) = 1

tsmall − tlarge

SW large (t) = 1

tsmall − tlarge

Here SW small (t), SW large (t) ∈ [0 , 1], SW small (t) = 0 for all t ≥ tsmall , SW large (t) = 0 for all t ≤ tlarge ,and SW small (t) + SW large (t) = 1 for all t. Moreover, we note that SW small (t), SW large (t) ∈ F  3, 2, 8, max {tsmall , (tsmall − tlarge )−1}, · .

Now we use the switching functions to construct the score network as 

es(x, t ) = SW small (t) · ¯ssmall (x, t ) + SW large (t) · ¯slarge (x, t ).

C.2 Proof of Theorem 5.2 

Built upon the score estimation results in Theorem 5.1, we aim to establish the convergence rate of the estimated distribution bP = Law( bYT −t0 ) in W1-distance. Firstly, we introduce { ¯Yt}T −t0 

> t=0

that replaces bY0 ∼ N (0 , I D) by ¯Y0 ∼ pT , i.e. d ¯Yt =

 12 ¯Yt + bs( ¯Yt, T − t)



dt + d Bt, ¯Y0 ∼ pT .

By the triangle inequality, we have the following decomposition, 

E[W1( bP , P data )] = E[W1( bYT −t0 , X 0)] ≤ E[W1( bYT −t0 , ¯YT −t0 )] + E[W1( ¯YT −t0 , Y T −t0 )] + E[W1(YT −t0 , X 0)] = E[W1( bYT −t0 , ¯YT −t0 )] + E[W1( ¯YT −t0 , Y T −t0 )] + E[W1(Xt0 , X 0)] .

55 Let X ∼ Pdata and Z ∼ N (0 , I D). Then we have

> t

. Moreover, combining Theorem D.7 in Oko et al. [2023] with Lemmas 4.5-4.6 and Theorem 5.1, we have E[W1( bYT −t0 , ¯YT −t0 )] ≲ exp( −T ), and 

E[W1( ¯YT −t0 , Y T −t0 )] ≲

Lemma E.1 (Jacobian matrix of the projection function) . Given a manifold M with reach r, for any x ∈ B (M, τ ), let Π( x) = argmin y∈M ∥x − y∥. We have 

∇xΠ( x)( x − Π( x)) = 0 

Proof. Firstly, we show that Π( x + ∆ x) − Π( x) = 0, where ∆ x ∈ RD is a small perturbation of x

belonging to the normal space of M at Π( x), and x, x + ∆ x ∈ B (M, τ ). By definition, we have 

∥(x + ∆ x) − Π( x + ∆ x)∥ = min  

> y∈M

∥(x + ∆ x) − y∥.

Here we decompose y − Π( x), for any y ∈ M , as y − Π( x) = u + v, where u ∈ RD lies in the tangent space of M at Π( x), and v ∈ RD lies in the normal space of M at Π( x). Due to orthogonality, we have min  

> y∈M

∥(x + ∆ x) − y∥ = min  

> Π( x)+ u+v∈M

∥(x + ∆ x) − (Π( x) + u + v)∥

= min  

> Π( x)+ u+v∈M

∥x − Π( x) + ∆ x − v∥ + ∥u∥

= min  

> Π( x)+ v∈M

∥x − Π( x) + ∆ x − v∥.

Note that Π( x) + v ∈ M if and only if v = 0, since v is in the normal space of M at Π( x) ∈ M .Therefore, we have 

∥(x + ∆ x) − Π( x + ∆ x)∥ = ∥x − Π( x) + ∆ x∥,

and thus Π( x + ∆ x) = Π( x). This yields ∇xΠ( x)∆ x = 0. Taking ∆ x = −x + Π( x) concludes the proof.

In this section, we provide the definition and properties of average Taylor polynomials. 

Definition F.1 (Averaged Taylor polynomials) . Suppose f ∈ Cα−1(Ω) where B(z0, ρ ) ⊆ Ω. The corresponding average Taylor polynomial of degree α of f averaged over B(z0, ρ ) is defined as

|θ|! (x − z)θ. (F.2) Here ϕ being arbitrary cut-off function satisfying 

ϕ ∈ C∞(Rd) with ϕ(z) ≥ 0 for all z ∈ Rd,

supp( ϕ) = B(z0, ρ ) and 

Z

> RD

ϕ(z)dz = 1 .

For f ∈ Cα(Ω), we define its Sobolev norm [Brenner, 2008, Definition 1.3.1] as ∥f ∥W α, ∞(Ω) =max |θ|≤ α ∥∂θf ∥L∞(Ω) with θ a multi-index. The averaged Taylor polynomial can approximate f

and its partial derivatives well. Specifically, Lemma F.2 provides an approximation guarantee in Sobolev norm. 

Lemma F.2 (Bramble-Hilbert, Chapter 4.1 in Brenner [2008]) . Suppose f ∈ Cα(B(z0, ρ )). There exists a constant Cα,d > 0 such that 

f − Qαz0 f W p, ∞(B(z0,ρ )) ≤ Cα,d ρα−p∥f ∥W α, ∞(B(z0,ρ )) for p = 0 , 1, . . . , α, 

where Qαz0 f denotes the averaged Taylor polynomial of degree α of f averaged over B(z0, ρ ). Lemma F.3 below shows that Qαz0 f can be written as a weighted sum of polynomials. 

Lemma F.3 (Proposition 4.1.12 of Brenner [2008]) . Let α ∈ N+ and f ∈ W α−1,∞(Ω). Let 

z0 ∈ Ω, ρ > 0 such that B(z0, ρ ) ⊆ Ω. Then the averaged Taylor polynomial Qαz0 (f ) can be written as
